background-color: rgb(235,235,210)"><a href="/wiki/Synonym_(taxonomy)" title="Synonym (taxonomy)">Synonyms</a>\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td colspan="2" style="text-align: left">\n<ul><li>Metazoa</li></ul>\n</td></tr>\n</tbody></table>\n<p><b>Animals</b> are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Multicellular" title="Multicellular">multicellular</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eukaryotic" title="Eukaryotic">eukaryotic</a> organisms that form the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_(biology)" title="Kingdom (biology)">biological kingdom</a> <b>Animalia</b>. With few exceptions, animals <a href="/wiki/Heterotroph" title="Heterotroph">consume organic material</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cellular_respiration#Aerobic_respiration" title="Cellular respiration">breathe oxygen</a>, are <a href="/wiki/Motility" title="Motility">able to move</a>, can <a href="/wiki/Sexual_reproduction" title="Sexual reproduction">reproduce sexually</a>, and grow from a hollow sphere of cells, the <a href="/wiki/Blastula" title="Blastula">blastula</a>, during <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Embryogenesis" title="Embryogenesis">embryonic development</a>. Over 1.5 million <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extant_taxon" title="Extant taxon">living</a> animal <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">species</a> have been <a href="/wiki/Species_description" title="Species description">described</a>&#8212;of which around 1 million are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Insecta" title="Insecta">insects</a>&#8212;but it has been estimated there are over 7 million animal species in total. Animals range in length from 8.5&#160;millionths of a metre to 33.6 metres (110&#160;ft) and have <a href="/wiki/Ecology" title="Ecology">complex interactions</a> with each other and their environments, forming intricate <a href="/wiki/Food_web" title="Food web">food webs</a>. The category includes <a href="/wiki/Human" title="Human">humans</a>, but in colloquial use the term <i>animal</i> often refers only to non-human animals. The study of non-human animals is known as <a href="/wiki/Zoology" title="Zoology">zoology</a>.\n</p><p>Most living animal species are in the <a href="/wiki/Bilateria" title="Bilateria">Bilateria</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Clade" title="Clade">clade</a> whose members have a <a href="/wiki/Symmetry_in_biology#Bilateral_symmetry" title="Symmetry in biology">bilaterally symmetric</a> body plan. The Bilateria include the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protostomes" title="Protostomes">protostomes</a>&#8212;in which many groups of <a href="/wiki/Invertebrate" title="Invertebrate">invertebrates</a> are found, such as <a href="/wiki/Nematode" title="Nematode">nematodes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arthropod" title="Arthropod">arthropods</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mollusc" title="Mollusc">molluscs</a>&#8212;and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deuterostomes" title="Deuterostomes">deuterostomes</a>, containing the <a href="/wiki/Echinoderm" title="Echinoderm">echinoderms</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chordate" title="Chordate">chordates</a> (including the <a href="/wiki/Vertebrate" title="Vertebrate">vertebrates</a>). Life forms interpreted as early animals were present in the <a href="/wiki/Ediacaran_biota" title="Ediacaran biota">Ediacaran biota</a> of the late <a href="/wiki/Precambrian" title="Precambrian">Precambrian</a>. Many modern animal phyla became clearly established in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fossil_record" title="Fossil record">fossil record</a> as <a href="/wiki/Marine_life" title="Marine life">marine species</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Cambrian_explosion" title="Cambrian explosion">Cambrian explosion</a> which began around 542 million years ago. 6,331 groups of <a href="/wiki/Gene" title="Gene">genes</a> common to all living animals have been identified; these may have arisen from a single <a href="/wiki/Animal#Phylogeny" title="Animal">common ancestor</a> that lived <a href="/wiki/Cryogenian" title="Cryogenian">650 million years ago</a>.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Aristotle%27s_biology" title="Aristotle\'s biology">Aristotle divided animals</a> into those with blood and those without. <a href="/wiki/Carl_Linnaeus" title="Carl Linnaeus">Carl Linnaeus</a> created the first hierarchical <a href="/wiki/Taxonomy_(biology)" title="Taxonomy (biology)">biological classification</a> for animals in 1758 with his <i><a href="/wiki/Systema_Naturae" title="Systema Naturae">Systema Naturae</a></i>, which <a href="/wiki/Jean-Baptiste_Lamarck" title="Jean-Baptiste Lamarck">Jean-Baptiste Lamarck</a> expanded into 14 <a href="/wiki/Phylum" title="Phylum">phyla</a> by 1809. In 1874, <a href="/wiki/Ernst_Haeckel" title="Ernst Haeckel">Ernst Haeckel</a> divided the animal kingdom into the multicellular <b>Metazoa</b> (now <a href="/wiki/Synonym_(taxonomy)" title="Synonym (taxonomy)">synonymous</a> with Animalia) and the <a href="/wiki/Protozoa" title="Protozoa">Protozoa</a>, single-celled organisms no longer considered animals. In modern times, the biological classification of animals relies on advanced techniques, such as <a href="/wiki/Molecular_phylogenetics" title="Molecular phylogenetics">molecular phylogenetics</a>, which are effective at demonstrating the evolutionary relationships between animal <a href="/wiki/Taxon" title="Taxon">taxa</a>.\n</p><p>Humans make use of many other animal species for food, including <a href="/wiki/Meat" title="Meat">meat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Milk" title="Milk">milk</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Egg" title="Egg">eggs</a>; for materials, such as <a href="/wiki/Leather" title="Leather">leather</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wool" title="Wool">wool</a>; as <a href="/wiki/Pet" title="Pet">pets</a>; and as <a href="/wiki/Working_animal" title="Working animal">working animals</a> for power and transport. Dogs have been <a href="/wiki/Hunting_dog" title="Hunting dog">used in hunting</a>, while many terrestrial and aquatic animals are hunted for sport. Non-human animals have appeared in art from the earliest times and are featured in mythology and religion.\n</p>\n<div class="toc" id="toc"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-1"><a href="#Etymology"><span class="tocnumber">1</span> <span class="toctext">Etymology</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-2"><a href="#Characteristics"><span class="tocnumber">2</span> <span class="toctext">Characteristics</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-3"><a href="#Structure"><span class="tocnumber">2.1</span> <span class="toctext">Structure</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-4"><a href="#Reproduction_and_development"><span class="tocnumber">2.2</span> <span class="toctext">Reproduction and development</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-5"><a href="#Ecology"><span class="tocnumber">3</span> <span class="toctext">Ecology</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-6"><a href="#Diversity"><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">Diversity</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-7"><a href="#Largest_and_smallest"><span class="tocnumber">4.1</span> <span class="toctext">Largest and smallest</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-8"><a href="#Numbers_and_habitats"><span class="tocnumber">4.2</span> <span class="toctext">Numbers and habitats</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-9"><a href="#Evolutionary_origin"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">Evolutionary origin</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-10"><a href="#Phylogeny"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Phylogeny</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-11"><a href="#Non-bilaterian_animals"><span class="tocnumber">6.1</span> <span class="toctext">Non-bilaterian animals</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-12"><a href="#Bilaterian_animals"><span class="tocnumber">6.2</span> <span class="toctext">Bilaterian animals</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-13"><a href="#Protostomes_and_deuterostomes"><span class="tocnumber">6.2.1</span> <span class="toctext">Protostomes and deuterostomes</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-4 tocsection-14"><a href="#Ecdysozoa"><span class="tocnumber">6.2.1.1</span> <span class="toctext">Ecdysozoa</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-4 tocsection-15"><a href="#Spiralia"><span class="tocnumber">6.2.1.2</span> <span class="toctext">Spiralia</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-16"><a href="#History_of_classification"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">History of classification</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-17"><a href="#In_human_culture"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">In human culture</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-18"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-19"><a href="#Notes"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">Notes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-20"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-21"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h2>\n<p>The word "animal" comes from the Latin <i><i lang="la" title="Latin language text"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/animalis#Latin" title="wikt:animalis">animalis</a></i></i>, meaning <i>having breath</i>, <i>having soul</i> or <i>living being</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> The biological definition includes all members of the kingdom Animalia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-americanheritage_animal_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-americanheritage_animal-2">[2]</a></sup> In colloquial usage, as a consequence of <a href="/wiki/Anthropocentrism" title="Anthropocentrism">anthropocentrism</a>, the term <i>animal</i> is sometimes used nonscientifically to refer only to non-human animals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Characteristics">Characteristics</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Blastulation.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="344" data-file-width="799" decoding="async" height="125" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Blastulation.png/290px-Blastulation.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Blastulation.png/435px-Blastulation.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Blastulation.png/580px-Blastulation.png 2x" width="290"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Blastulation.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Animals are unique in having the ball of cells of the early <a href="/wiki/Embryo" title="Embryo">embryo</a> (1) develop into a hollow ball or <a href="/wiki/Blastula" title="Blastula">blastula</a> (2).</div></div></div>\n<p>Animals have several characteristics that set them apart from other living things. Animals are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eukaryotic" title="Eukaryotic">eukaryotic</a> and multicellular,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Avila1995_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Avila1995-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-palaeos_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-palaeos-8">[8]</a></sup> unlike bacteria, which are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prokaryotic" title="Prokaryotic">prokaryotic</a>, and unlike <a href="/wiki/Protist" title="Protist">protists</a>, which are eukaryotic but <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Unicellular" title="Unicellular">unicellular</a>. Unlike plants and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alga" title="Alga">algae</a>, which <a href="/wiki/Autotroph" title="Autotroph">produce their own nutrients</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AnimalCells_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-AnimalCells-9">[9]</a></sup> animals are <a href="/wiki/Heterotroph" title="Heterotroph">heterotrophic</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-palaeos_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-palaeos-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Windows_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Windows-10">[10]</a></sup> feeding on organic material and digesting it internally.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> With very few exceptions, animals breathe oxygen and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aerobic_respiration" title="Aerobic respiration">respire aerobically</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oxygen_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-oxygen-12">[12]</a></sup> All animals are <a href="/wiki/Motility" title="Motility">motile</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Concepts_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Concepts-13">[13]</a></sup> (able to spontaneously move their bodies) during at least part of their <a href="/wiki/Biological_life_cycle" title="Biological life cycle">life cycle</a>, but some animals, such as <a href="/wiki/Sponge" title="Sponge">sponges</a>, <a href="/wiki/Coral" title="Coral">corals</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mussel" title="Mussel">mussels</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Barnacle" title="Barnacle">barnacles</a>, later become <a href="/wiki/Sessility_(motility)" title="Sessility (motility)">sessile</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Blastula" title="Blastula">blastula</a> is a stage in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Embryogenesis" title="Embryogenesis">embryonic development</a> that is unique to most animals,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> allowing <a href="/wiki/Cellular_differentiation" title="Cellular differentiation">cells to be differentiated</a> into specialised tissues and organs.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Structure">Structure</span></h3>\n<p>All animals are composed of cells, surrounded by a characteristic <a href="/wiki/Extracellular_matrix" title="Extracellular matrix">extracellular matrix</a> composed of <a href="/wiki/Collagen" title="Collagen">collagen</a> and elastic <a href="/wiki/Glycoprotein" title="Glycoprotein">glycoproteins</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> During development, the animal extracellular matrix forms a relatively flexible framework upon which cells can move about and be reorganised, making the formation of complex structures possible. This may be calcified, forming structures such as <a href="/wiki/Exoskeleton" title="Exoskeleton">shells</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bone" title="Bone">bones</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spicule_(sponge)" title="Spicule (sponge)">spicules</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> In contrast, the cells of other multicellular organisms (primarily algae, plants, and fungi) are held in place by cell walls, and so develop by progressive growth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> Animal cells uniquely possess the <a href="/wiki/Cell_junction" title="Cell junction">cell junctions</a> called <a href="/wiki/Tight_junction" title="Tight junction">tight junctions</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gap_junction" title="Gap junction">gap junctions</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Desmosome" title="Desmosome">desmosomes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup>\n</p><p>With few exceptions&#8212;in particular, the sponges and <a href="/wiki/Placozoa" title="Placozoa">placozoans</a>&#8212;animal bodies are differentiated into <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biological_tissue" title="Biological tissue">tissues</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> These include <a href="/wiki/Muscle" title="Muscle">muscles</a>, which enable locomotion, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nerve_tissue" title="Nerve tissue">nerve tissues</a>, which transmit signals and coordinate the body. Typically, there is also an internal <a href="/wiki/Digestion" title="Digestion">digestive</a> chamber with either one opening (as in flatworms) or two openings (as in deuterostomes).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reproduction_and_development">Reproduction and development</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Sexual_reproduction#Animals" title="Sexual reproduction">Sexual reproduction &#167;&#160;Animals</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Asexual_reproduction#Examples_in_animals" title="Asexual reproduction">Asexual reproduction &#167;&#160;Examples in animals</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Odonata_copulation.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1359" data-file-width="2117" decoding="async" height="141" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Odonata_copulation.jpg/220px-Odonata_copulation.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Odonata_copulation.jpg/330px-Odonata_copulation.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Odonata_copulation.jpg/440px-Odonata_copulation.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Odonata_copulation.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Sexual_reproduction" title="Sexual reproduction">Sexual reproduction</a> is nearly universal in animals, such as these <a href="/wiki/Dragonfly" title="Dragonfly">dragonflies</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>Nearly all animals make use of some form of sexual reproduction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> They produce <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Haploid" title="Haploid">haploid</a> <a href="/wiki/Gamete" title="Gamete">gametes</a> by <a href="/wiki/Meiosis" title="Meiosis">meiosis</a>; the smaller, motile gametes are <a href="/wiki/Spermatozoon" title="Spermatozoon">spermatozoa</a> and the larger, non-motile gametes are <a href="/wiki/Egg_cell" title="Egg cell">ova</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> These fuse to form <a href="/wiki/Zygote" title="Zygote">zygotes</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> which develop via <a href="/wiki/Mitosis" title="Mitosis">mitosis</a> into a hollow sphere, called a blastula. In sponges, blastula larvae swim to a new location, attach to the seabed, and develop into a new sponge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> In most other groups, the blastula  undergoes more complicated rearrangement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> It first <a href="/wiki/Invagination" title="Invagination">invaginates</a> to form a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gastrula" title="Gastrula">gastrula</a> with a digestive chamber and two separate <a href="/wiki/Germ_layer" title="Germ layer">germ layers</a>, an external <a href="/wiki/Ectoderm" title="Ectoderm">ectoderm</a> and an internal <a href="/wiki/Endoderm" title="Endoderm">endoderm</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> In most cases, a third germ layer, the <a href="/wiki/Mesoderm" title="Mesoderm">mesoderm</a>, also develops between them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> These germ layers then differentiate to form tissues and organs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Repeated instances of <a href="/wiki/Inbreeding" title="Inbreeding">mating with a close relative</a> during sexual reproduction generally leads to <a href="/wiki/Inbreeding_depression" title="Inbreeding depression">inbreeding depression</a> within a population due to the increased prevalence of harmful <a href="/wiki/Dominance_(genetics)" title="Dominance (genetics)">recessive</a> traits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid19834483_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid19834483-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid3324702_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid3324702-30">[30]</a></sup> Animals have evolved numerous mechanisms for <a href="/wiki/Inbreeding_avoidance" title="Inbreeding avoidance">avoiding close inbreeding</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pusey_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pusey-31">[31]</a></sup> In some species, such as the <a href="/wiki/Splendid_fairywren" title="Splendid fairywren">splendid fairywren</a> (<i>Malurus splendens</i>), females benefit by mating with multiple males, thus producing more offspring of higher genetic quality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Petrie_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Petrie-32">[32]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Some animals are capable of <a href="/wiki/Asexual_reproduction" title="Asexual reproduction">asexual reproduction</a>, which often results in a genetic clone of the parent. This may take place through <a href="/wiki/Fragmentation_(reproduction)" title="Fragmentation (reproduction)">fragmentation</a>; <a href="/wiki/Budding" title="Budding">budding</a>, such as in <a href="/wiki/Hydra_(genus)" title="Hydra (genus)"><i>Hydra</i></a> and other <a href="/wiki/Cnidaria" title="Cnidaria">cnidarians</a>; or <a href="/wiki/Parthenogenesis" title="Parthenogenesis">parthenogenesis</a>, where fertile eggs are produced without <a href="/wiki/Mating" title="Mating">mating</a>, such as in <a href="/wiki/Aphid" title="Aphid">aphids</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ecology">Ecology</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ultramarine_Flycatcher_(Ficedula_superciliaris)_Naggar,_Himachal_Pradesh,_2013_(cropped).JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1781" data-file-width="1383" decoding="async" height="219" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Ultramarine_Flycatcher_%28Ficedula_superciliaris%29_Naggar%2C_Himachal_Pradesh%2C_2013_%28cropped%29.JPG/170px-Ultramarine_Flycatcher_%28Ficedula_superciliaris%29_Naggar%2C_Himachal_Pradesh%2C_2013_%28cropped%29.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Ultramarine_Flycatcher_%28Ficedula_superciliaris%29_Naggar%2C_Himachal_Pradesh%2C_2013_%28cropped%29.JPG/255px-Ultramarine_Flycatcher_%28Ficedula_superciliaris%29_Naggar%2C_Himachal_Pradesh%2C_2013_%28cropped%29.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Ultramarine_Flycatcher_%28Ficedula_superciliaris%29_Naggar%2C_Himachal_Pradesh%2C_2013_%28cropped%29.JPG/340px-Ultramarine_Flycatcher_%28Ficedula_superciliaris%29_Naggar%2C_Himachal_Pradesh%2C_2013_%28cropped%29.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ultramarine_Flycatcher_(Ficedula_superciliaris)_Naggar,_Himachal_Pradesh,_2013_(cropped).JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Predators, such as this <a href="/wiki/Ultramarine_flycatcher" title="Ultramarine flycatcher">ultramarine flycatcher</a> (<i>Ficedula superciliaris</i>), feed on other organisms.</div></div></div>\n<p>Animals are categorised into <a href="/wiki/Ecology" title="Ecology">ecological</a> groups depending on how they obtain or consume organic material, including <a href="/wiki/Carnivore" title="Carnivore">carnivores</a>, <a href="/wiki/Herbivore" title="Herbivore">herbivores</a>, <a href="/wiki/Omnivore" title="Omnivore">omnivores</a>, <a href="/wiki/Detritivore" title="Detritivore">detritivores</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parasite" title="Parasite">parasites</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> Interactions between animals form complex <a href="/wiki/Food_web" title="Food web">food webs</a>. In carnivorous or omnivorous species, <a href="/wiki/Predation" title="Predation">predation</a> is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Consumer-resource_systems" title="Consumer-resource systems">consumer-resource interaction</a> where a predator feeds on another organism (called its <i>prey</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ecology_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ecology-37">[37]</a></sup> Selective pressures imposed on one another lead to an <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_arms_race" title="Evolutionary arms race">evolutionary arms race</a> between predator and prey, resulting in various <a href="/wiki/Anti-predator_adaptation" title="Anti-predator adaptation">anti-predator adaptations</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> Almost all multicellular predators are animals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SimpsonCB_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-SimpsonCB-40">[40]</a></sup> Some <a href="/wiki/Consumer_(food_chain)" title="Consumer (food chain)">consumers</a> use multiple methods; for example, in <a href="/wiki/Parasitoid_wasp" title="Parasitoid wasp">parasitoid wasps</a>, the larvae feed on the hosts\' living tissues, killing them in the process,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> but the adults primarily consume nectar from flowers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> Other animals may have very specific <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Feeding_behaviour" title="Feeding behaviour">feeding behaviours</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Hawksbill_sea_turtle" title="Hawksbill sea turtle">hawksbill sea turtles</a> that primarily <a href="/wiki/Spongivore" title="Spongivore">eat sponges</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ScienceSpongi_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-ScienceSpongi-43">[43]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Expl0072_-_Flickr_-_NOAA_Photo_Library.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1920" data-file-width="2560" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Expl0072_-_Flickr_-_NOAA_Photo_Library.jpg/220px-Expl0072_-_Flickr_-_NOAA_Photo_Library.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Expl0072_-_Flickr_-_NOAA_Photo_Library.jpg/330px-Expl0072_-_Flickr_-_NOAA_Photo_Library.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Expl0072_-_Flickr_-_NOAA_Photo_Library.jpg/440px-Expl0072_-_Flickr_-_NOAA_Photo_Library.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Expl0072_-_Flickr_-_NOAA_Photo_Library.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Hydrothermal_vent" title="Hydrothermal vent">Hydrothermal vent</a> mussels and shrimps</div></div></div>\n<p>Most animals rely on the energy produced by plants through <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthesis</a>. Herbivores eat plant material directly, while carnivores, and other animals on higher <a href="/wiki/Trophic_level" title="Trophic level">trophic levels</a>, typically acquire energy (in the form of <a href="/wiki/Redox" title="Redox">reduced</a> carbon) by eating other animals. The <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate" title="Carbohydrate">carbohydrates</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lipid" title="Lipid">lipids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">proteins</a>, and other biomolecules are broken down to allow the animal to grow and to sustain biological processes such as <a href="/wiki/Animal_locomotion" title="Animal locomotion">locomotion</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> Animals living close to <a href="/wiki/Hydrothermal_vent" title="Hydrothermal vent">hydrothermal vents</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cold_seep" title="Cold seep">cold seeps</a> on the dark <a href="/wiki/Seabed" title="Seabed">sea floor</a> do not depend on the energy of sunlight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> Rather, <a href="/wiki/Archaea" title="Archaea">archaea</a> and bacteria in these locations produce organic matter through <a href="/wiki/Chemosynthesis" title="Chemosynthesis">chemosynthesis</a> (by oxidizing inorganic compounds, such as methane) and form the base of the local food web.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Animals originally evolved in the sea. Lineages of arthropods colonised land around the same time as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Land_plant" title="Land plant">land plants</a>, probably between 510&#8211;471 million years ago during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Late_Cambrian" title="Late Cambrian">Late Cambrian</a> or Early <a href="/wiki/Ordovician" title="Ordovician">Ordovician</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rota-Stabelli2013_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rota-Stabelli2013-49">[49]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Vertebrate" title="Vertebrate">Vertebrates</a> such as the <a href="/wiki/Sarcopterygii" title="Sarcopterygii">lobe-finned fish</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Tiktaalik" title="Tiktaalik">Tiktaalik</a></i> started to move on to land in the late <a href="/wiki/Devonian" title="Devonian">Devonian</a>, about 375 million years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> Animals occupy virtually all of earth\'s <a href="/wiki/Habitat" title="Habitat">habitats</a> and microhabitats, including salt water, hydrothermal vents, fresh water, hot springs, swamps, forests, pastures, deserts, air, and the interiors of animals, plants, fungi and rocks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Margulis_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Margulis-52">[52]</a></sup> Animals are however not particularly <a href="/wiki/Thermophile" title="Thermophile">heat tolerant</a>; very few of them can survive at constant temperatures above 50&#160;&#176;C (122&#160;&#176;F).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clarke2014_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-Clarke2014-53">[53]</a></sup> Only very few species of animals (mostly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nematodes" title="Nematodes">nematodes</a>) inhabit the most extreme cold deserts of continental <a href="/wiki/Antarctica" title="Antarctica">Antarctica</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bas-land_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-bas-land-54">[54]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Diversity">Diversity</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Anim1754_-_Flickr_-_NOAA_Photo_Library_(1).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1950" data-file-width="2600" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Anim1754_-_Flickr_-_NOAA_Photo_Library_%281%29.jpg/220px-Anim1754_-_Flickr_-_NOAA_Photo_Library_%281%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Anim1754_-_Flickr_-_NOAA_Photo_Library_%281%29.jpg/330px-Anim1754_-_Flickr_-_NOAA_Photo_Library_%281%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Anim1754_-_Flickr_-_NOAA_Photo_Library_%281%29.jpg/440px-Anim1754_-_Flickr_-_NOAA_Photo_Library_%281%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Anim1754_-_Flickr_-_NOAA_Photo_Library_(1).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Blue_whale" title="Blue whale">blue whale</a> is the largest animal that has ever lived.</div></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Largest_and_smallest">Largest and smallest</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Largest_organisms" title="Largest organisms">Largest organisms</a> and <a href="/wiki/Smallest_organisms" title="Smallest organisms">Smallest organisms</a></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Blue_whale" title="Blue whale">blue whale</a> (<i>Balaenoptera musculus</i>) is the largest animal that has ever lived, weighing up to 190 metric <a href="/wiki/Tonne" title="Tonne">tonnes</a> and measuring up to 33.6 metres (110&#160;ft) long.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wood_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wood-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> The largest extant terrestrial animal is the <a href="/wiki/African_bush_elephant" title="African bush elephant">African bush elephant</a> (<i>Loxodonta africana</i>), weighing up to 12.25 tonnes<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wood_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wood-55">[55]</a></sup> and measuring up to 10.67 metres (35.0&#160;ft) long.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wood_55-2"><a href="#cite_note-Wood-55">[55]</a></sup> The largest terrestrial animals that ever lived were <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Titanosaur" title="Titanosaur">titanosaur</a> <a href="/wiki/Sauropoda" title="Sauropoda">sauropod dinosaurs</a> such as <i><a href="/wiki/Argentinosaurus" title="Argentinosaurus">Argentinosaurus</a></i>, which may have weighed as much as 73 tonnes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mazzettaetal2004_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mazzettaetal2004-58">[58]</a></sup> Several animals are microscopic; some <a href="/wiki/Myxozoa" title="Myxozoa">Myxozoa</a> (<a href="/wiki/Obligate_parasite" title="Obligate parasite">obligate parasites</a> within the Cnidaria) never grow larger than 20&#160;<a href="/wiki/Micrometre" title="Micrometre">&#181;m</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> and one of the smallest species (<i>Myxobolus shekel</i>) is no more than 8.5&#160;&#181;m when fully grown.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Numbers_and_habitats">Numbers and habitats</span></h3>\n<p>The following table lists estimated numbers of described extant species for the animal groups with the largest numbers of species,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zhang2013_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zhang2013-61">[61]</a></sup> along with their principal habitats (terrestrial, fresh water,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Balian2008_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Balian2008-62">[62]</a></sup> and marine),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hogenboom2016_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hogenboom2016-63">[63]</a></sup> and free-living or parasitic ways of life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Poulin2007_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Poulin2007-64">[64]</a></sup> Species estimates shown here are based on numbers described scientifically; much larger estimates have been calculated based on various means of prediction, and these can vary wildly. For instance, around 25,000&#8211;27,000 species of nematodes have been described, while published estimates of the total number of nematode species include 10,000&#8211;20,000; 500,000; 10 million; and 100 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Felder2009_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Felder2009-65">[65]</a></sup> Using patterns within the <a href="/wiki/Taxonomy_(biology)" title="Taxonomy (biology)">taxonomic</a> hierarchy, the total number of animal species&#8212;including those not yet described&#8212;was calculated to be about 7.77 million in 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mora2011_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mora2011-67">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[a]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable">\n<tbody><tr>\n<th><a href="/wiki/Phylum" title="Phylum">Phylum</a>\n</th>\n<th>Example\n</th>\n<th>No. of<br/>Species\n</th>\n<th><a //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Grapevinesnail_01.jpg/200px-Grapevinesnail_01.jpg 2x" width="100"/></a>\n</td>\n<td align="right">85,000<sup class="reference" href="#cite_note-Balian2008-62">[62]</a></sup>\n</td>\n<td>Yes\n</td>\n<td>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><b><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sponges" title="Sponges">Sponges</a></b>\n</td>\n<td><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:A_colourful_Sponge_on_the_Fathom.jpg"><img alt="A colourful Sponge on the Fathom.jpg" data-file-height="831" data-file-width="1216" decoding="async" height="68" href="#cite_note-Hogenboom2016-63">[63]</a></sup>\n</td>\n<td>200-300<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Balian2008_62-9"><a class="center" style="width:auto; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;">Total number of described species as of  2013<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Animal&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>: 1,525,728<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zhang2013_61-13"><a href="#cite_note-Zhang2013-61">[61]</a></sup></div>\n</th></tr></tbody></table>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Evolutionary_origin">Evolutionary origin</span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Urmetazoan" title="Urmetazoan">Urmetazoan</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:DickinsoniaCostata.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1704" data-file-width="2272" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/DickinsoniaCostata.jpg/220px-DickinsoniaCostata.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/DickinsoniaCostata.jpg/330px-DickinsoniaCostata.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/DickinsoniaCostata.jpg/440px-DickinsoniaCostata.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:DickinsoniaCostata.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Dickinsonia" title="Dickinsonia">Dickinsonia costata</a></i> from the <a href="/wiki/Ediacaran_biota" title="Ediacaran biota">Ediacaran biota</a> (c. 635&#8211;542 MYA) is one of the earliest animal species known.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bobrovskiy_Hope_Ivantsov_Nettersheim_pp._1246&#8211;1249_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bobrovskiy_Hope_Ivantsov_Nettersheim_pp._1246&#8211;1249-79">[77]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>The first <a href="/wiki/Fossil" title="Fossil">fossils</a> that might represent animals appear in the 665-million-year-old rocks of the <a href="/wiki/Trezona_Formation" title="Trezona Formation">Trezona Formation</a> of <a href="/wiki/South_Australia" title="South Australia">South Australia</a>. These fossils are interpreted as most probably being early <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Porifera" title="Porifera">sponges</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-roseMaloof_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-roseMaloof-80">[78]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The oldest animals are found in the <a href="/wiki/Ediacaran_biota" title="Ediacaran biota">Ediacaran biota</a>, towards the end of the Precambrian, around 610 million years ago. It had long been doubtful whether these included animals,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[81]</a></sup> but the discovery of the animal lipid <a href="/wiki/Cholesterol" title="Cholesterol">cholesterol</a> in fossils of <i><a href="/wiki/Dickinsonia" title="Dickinsonia">Dickinsonia</a></i> establishes that these were indeed animals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bobrovskiy_Hope_Ivantsov_Nettersheim_pp._1246&#8211;1249_79-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bobrovskiy_Hope_Ivantsov_Nettersheim_pp._1246&#8211;1249-79">[77]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Anomalocaris_NT_small.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="373" data-file-width="483" decoding="async" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Anomalocaris_NT_small.jpg/220px-Anomalocaris_NT_small.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Anomalocaris_NT_small.jpg/330px-Anomalocaris_NT_small.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Anomalocaris_NT_small.jpg/440px-Anomalocaris_NT_small.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Anomalocaris_NT_small.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anomalocaris_canadensis" title="Anomalocaris canadensis">Anomalocaris canadensis</a></i> is one of the many animal species that emerged in the <a href="/wiki/Cambrian_explosion" title="Cambrian explosion">Cambrian explosion</a>, starting some 542 million years ago, and found in the fossil beds of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Burgess_shale" title="Burgess shale">Burgess shale</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>Many animal phyla first appear in the fossil record during the <a href="/wiki/Cambrian_explosion" title="Cambrian explosion">Cambrian explosion</a>, starting about 542 million years ago, in beds such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Burgess_shale" title="Burgess shale">Burgess shale</a>. Extant phyla in these rocks include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mollusc" title="Mollusc">molluscs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brachiopod" title="Brachiopod">brachiopods</a>, <a href="/wiki/Onychophora" title="Onychophora">onychophorans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tardigrade" title="Tardigrade">tardigrades</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arthropod" title="Arthropod">arthropods</a>, <a href="/wiki/Echinoderm" title="Echinoderm">echinoderms</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hemichordate" title="Hemichordate">hemichordates</a>, along with numerous now-extinct forms such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Predator" title="Predator">predatory</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Anomalocaris" title="Anomalocaris">Anomalocaris</a></i>. The apparent suddenness of the event may however be an artefact of the fossil record, rather than showing that all these animals appeared simultaneously.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Royal_Ontario_Museum_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-Royal_Ontario_Museum-87">[85]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Some palaeontologists have suggested that animals appeared much earlier than the Cambrian explosion, possibly as early as 1 billion years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[86]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Trace_fossil" title="Trace fossil">Trace fossils</a> such as tracks and burrows found in the <a href="/wiki/Tonian" title="Tonian">Tonian</a> period may indicate the presence of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Triploblastic" title="Triploblastic">triploblastic</a> worm-like animals, roughly as large (about 5&#160;mm wide) and complex as earthworms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Seilacher1998_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-Seilacher1998-89">[87]</a></sup> However, similar tracks are produced today by the giant single-celled protist <i><a href="/wiki/Gromia_sphaerica" title="Gromia sphaerica">Gromia sphaerica</a></i>, so the Tonian trace fossils may not indicate early animal evolution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Matz2008_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-Matz2008-90">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MSNBC200811_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-MSNBC200811-91">[89]</a></sup> Around the same time, another line of evidence may indicate the appearance of grazing animals: the layered mats of <a href="/wiki/Microorganism" title="Microorganism">microorganisms</a> called <a href="/wiki/Stromatolite" title="Stromatolite">stromatolites</a> decreased in diversity, perhaps due to grazing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bengtson2002OriginsOfPredation_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bengtson2002OriginsOfPredation-92">[90]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Phylogeny">Phylogeny</span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Lists_of_animals" title="Lists of animals">Lists of animals</a></div>\n<p>Animals are <a href="/wiki/Monophyly" title="Monophyly">monophyletic</a>, meaning they are derived from a common ancestor. Animals are sister to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Choanoflagellata" title="Choanoflagellata">Choanoflagellata</a>, with which they form the <a href="/wiki/Choanozoa" title="Choanozoa">Choanozoa</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Budd2015_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-Budd2015-93">[91]</a></sup> The most <a href="/wiki/Basal_(phylogenetics)" title="Basal (phylogenetics)">basal</a> animals, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Porifera" title="Porifera">Porifera</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ctenophora" title="Ctenophora">Ctenophora</a>, Cnidaria, and Placozoa, have body plans that lack <a href="/wiki/Symmetry_in_biology" title="Symmetry in biology">bilateral symmetry</a>. Their relationships are still disputed; the sister group to all other animals could be the Porifera or the Ctenophora, which like the Porifera lack <a href="/wiki/Hox_gene" title="Hox gene">hox genes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_developmental_biology#Gene_toolkit" title="Evolutionary developmental biology">important in body plan development</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Giribet_2016_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-Giribet_2016-94">[92]</a></sup>\n</p><p>These genes are found in the Placozoa<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dellaporta2004_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dellaporta2004-96">[94]</a></sup> and the higher animals, the Bilateria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peterson2001_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-Peterson2001-97">[95]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KraemerEis2016_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-KraemerEis2016-98">[96]</a></sup> 6,331 groups of <a href="/wiki/Gene" title="Gene">genes</a> common to all living animals have been identified; these may have arisen from a single <a href="/wiki/Animal#Phylogeny" title="Animal">common ancestor</a> that lived <a href="/wiki/Cryogenian" title="Cryogenian">650 million years ago</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Precambrian" title="Precambrian">Precambrian</a>. 25 of these are novel core gene groups, found only in animals; of those, 8 are for essential components of the <a href="/wiki/Wnt_signaling_pathway" title="Wnt signaling pathway">Wnt</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/TGF-beta" title="TGF-beta">TGF-beta</a> signalling pathways which may have enabled animals to become multicellular by providing a pattern for the body\'s system of axes (in three dimensions), and another 7 are for <a href="/wiki/Transcription_factor" title="Transcription factor">transcription factors</a> including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homeodomain" title="Homeodomain">homeodomain</a> proteins involved in the <a href="/wiki/Evo-devo_gene_toolkit" title="Evo-devo gene toolkit">control of development</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zimmer2018_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zimmer2018-99">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Paps2018_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-Paps2018-100">[98]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Phylogenetic_tree" title="Phylogenetic tree">phylogenetic tree</a> (of major lineages only) indicates approximately how many millions of years ago (<em>mya</em>) the lineages split.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[103]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r853264556">.mw-parser-output
