class="clade-slabel">760&#160;mya\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td class="clade-slabel">950&#160;mya\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><span id="Ctenophora.2C_Porifera.2C_Placozoa.2C_Cnidaria_and_Bilateria"></span>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Non-bilaterian_animals">Non-bilaterian animals</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Elephant-ear-sponge.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1214" data-file-width="835" decoding="async" height="247" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Elephant-ear-sponge.jpg/170px-Elephant-ear-sponge.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Elephant-ear-sponge.jpg/255px-Elephant-ear-sponge.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Elephant-ear-sponge.jpg/340px-Elephant-ear-sponge.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Elephant-ear-sponge.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Non-bilaterians include sponges (centre) and corals (background).</div></div></div>\n<p>Several animal phyla lack bilateral symmetry. Among these, the sponges (Porifera) probably diverged first, representing the oldest animal phylum.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[104]</a></sup> Sponges lack the complex organization found in most other animal phyla;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[105]</a></sup> their cells are differentiated, but in most cases not organised into distinct tissues.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[106]</a></sup> They typically feed by drawing in water through pores.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[107]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Ctenophora (comb jellies) and Cnidaria (which includes <a href="/wiki/Jellyfish" title="Jellyfish">jellyfish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sea_anemone" title="Sea anemone">sea anemones</a>, and corals) are radially symmetric and have digestive chambers with a single opening, which serves as both mouth and anus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[108]</a></sup> Animals in both phyla have distinct tissues, but these are not organised into <a href="/wiki/Organ_(anatomy)" title="Organ (anatomy)">organs</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[109]</a></sup> They are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diploblastic" title="Diploblastic">diploblastic</a>, having only two main germ layers, ectoderm and endoderm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[110]</a></sup> The tiny <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Placozoan" title="Placozoan">placozoans</a> are similar, but they do not have a permanent digestive chamber.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[111]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[112]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bilaterian_animals">Bilaterian animals</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Bilateria" title="Bilateria">Bilateria</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Symmetry_(biology)#Bilateral_symmetry" title="Symmetry (biology)">Symmetry (biology) &#167;&#160;Bilateral symmetry</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:372px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bilaterian_body_plan.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="581" data-file-width="1185" decoding="async" height="181" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Bilaterian_body_plan.svg/370px-Bilaterian_body_plan.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Bilaterian_body_plan.svg/555px-Bilaterian_body_plan.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Bilaterian_body_plan.svg/740px-Bilaterian_body_plan.svg.png 2x" width="370"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bilaterian_body_plan.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Idealised <a href="/wiki/Bilateria" title="Bilateria">bilaterian</a> body plan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[c]</a></sup> With an elongated body and a direction of movement the animal has head and tail ends. Sense organs and mouth form the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cephalisation" title="Cephalisation">basis of the head</a>. Opposed circular and longitudinal muscles enable <a href="/wiki/Peristalsis" title="Peristalsis">peristaltic motion</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>The remaining animals, the great majority&#8212;comprising some 29 phyla and over a million species&#8212;form a clade, the Bilateria. The body is <a href="/wiki/Triploblasty" title="Triploblasty">triploblastic</a>, with three well-developed germ layers, and their tissues <a href="/wiki/Organogenesis" title="Organogenesis">form distinct organs</a>. The digestive chamber has two openings, a mouth and an anus, and there is an internal body cavity, a <a href="/wiki/Coelom" title="Coelom">coelom</a> or pseudocoelom. Animals with this bilaterally symmetric <a href="/wiki/Body_plan" title="Body plan">body plan</a> and a tendency to move in one direction have a head end (anterior) and a tail end (posterior) as well as a back (dorsal) and a belly (ventral); therefore they also have a left side and a right side.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Minelli2009_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-Minelli2009-116">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brusca2016_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brusca2016-117">[114]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Having a front end means that this part of the body encounters stimuli, such as food, favouring <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cephalisation" title="Cephalisation">cephalisation</a>, the development of a head with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sense_organ" title="Sense organ">sense organs</a> and a mouth. Many bilaterians have a combination of circular <a href="/wiki/Muscle" title="Muscle">muscles</a> that constrict the body, making it longer, and an opposing set of longitudinal muscles, that shorten the body;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brusca2016_117-1"><a href="#cite_note-Brusca2016-117">[114]</a></sup> these enable soft-bodied animals with a <a href="/wiki/Hydrostatic_skeleton" title="Hydrostatic skeleton">hydrostatic skeleton</a> to move by <a href="/wiki/Peristalsis" title="Peristalsis">peristalsis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Quillin_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-Quillin-118">[115]</a></sup> They also have a gut that extends through the basically cylindrical body from mouth to anus. Many bilaterian phyla have primary <a href="/wiki/Larva" title="Larva">larvae</a> which swim with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cilia" title="Cilia">cilia</a> and have an apical organ containing sensory cells. However, there are exceptions to each of these characteristics; for example, adult echinoderms are radially symmetric (unlike their larvae), while some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Helminths" title="Helminths">parasitic worms</a> have extremely simplified body structures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Minelli2009_116-1"><a href="#cite_note-Minelli2009-116">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brusca2016_117-2"><a href="#cite_note-Brusca2016-117">[114]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Genetic studies have considerably changed zoologists\' understanding of the relationships within the Bilateria. Most appear to belong to two major lineages, the  <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protostomes" title="Protostomes">protostomes</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deuterostomes" title="Deuterostomes">deuterostomes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Telford_2008_pp._457&#8211;459_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-Telford_2008_pp._457&#8211;459-119">[116]</a></sup> The basalmost bilaterians are the <a href="/wiki/Xenacoelomorpha" title="Xenacoelomorpha">Xenacoelomorpha</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Philippe2011_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-Philippe2011-120">[117]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Perseke2007_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-Perseke2007-121">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cannon_2016_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cannon_2016-122">[119]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Protostomes_and_deuterostomes">Protostomes and deuterostomes</span></h4>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Embryological_origins_of_the_mouth_and_anus" title="Embryological origins of the mouth and anus">Embryological origins of the mouth and anus</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Protovsdeuterostomes.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="527" data-file-width="577" decoding="async" height="265" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Protovsdeuterostomes.svg/290px-Protovsdeuterostomes.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Protovsdeuterostomes.svg/435px-Protovsdeuterostomes.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Protovsdeuterostomes.svg/580px-Protovsdeuterostomes.svg.png 2x" width="290"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Protovsdeuterostomes.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The bilaterian gut develops in two ways. In many <a href="/wiki/Protostome" title="Protostome">protostomes</a>, the blastopore develops into the mouth, while in <a href="/wiki/Deuterostome" title="Deuterostome">deuterostomes</a> it becomes the anus.</div></div></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Protostome" title="Protostome">Protostome</a> and <a href="/wiki/Deuterostome" title="Deuterostome">Deuterostome</a></div>\n<p>Protostomes and deuterostomes differ in several ways. Early in development, deuterostome embryos undergo radial <a href="/wiki/Cleavage_(embryo)" title="Cleavage (embryo)">cleavage</a> during cell division, while many protostomes (the <a href="/wiki/Spiralia" title="Spiralia">Spiralia</a>) undergo spiral cleavage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[120]</a></sup>\nAnimals from both groups possess a complete digestive tract, but in protostomes the first opening of the <a href="/wiki/Archenteron" title="Archenteron">embryonic gut</a> develops into the mouth, and the anus forms secondarily. In deuterostomes, the anus forms first while the mouth develops secondarily.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[121]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hejnol2009_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hejnol2009-125">[122]</a></sup> Most protostomes have <a href="/wiki/Schizocoely" title="Schizocoely">schizocoelous development</a>, where cells simply fill in the interior of the gastrula to form the mesoderm. In deuterostomes, the mesoderm forms by <a href="/wiki/Enterocoely" title="Enterocoely">enterocoelic pouching</a>, through invagination of the endoderm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[123]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The main deuterostome phyla are the Echinodermata and the Chordata.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[124]</a></sup> Echinoderms are exclusively marine and include <a href="/wiki/Starfish" title="Starfish">starfish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sea_urchin" title="Sea urchin">sea urchins</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sea_cucumber" title="Sea cucumber">sea cucumbers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[125]</a></sup> The chordates are dominated by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vertebrates" title="Vertebrates">vertebrates</a> (animals with <a href="/wiki/Vertebral_column" title="Vertebral column">backbones</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[126]</a></sup> which consist of <a href="/wiki/Fish" title="Fish">fishes</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amphibia" title="Amphibia">amphibians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Reptile" title="Reptile">reptiles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bird" title="Bird">birds</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">mammals</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[127]</a></sup> The deuterostomes also include the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hemichordata" title="Hemichordata">Hemichordata</a> (acorn worms).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[128]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[129]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Ecdysozoa">Ecdysozoa</span></h5>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Anax_Imperator_2(loz).JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3754" data-file-width="2502" decoding="async" height="255" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Anax_Imperator_2%28loz%29.JPG/170px-Anax_Imperator_2%28loz%29.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Anax_Imperator_2%28loz%29.JPG/255px-Anax_Imperator_2%28loz%29.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Anax_Imperator_2%28loz%29.JPG/340px-Anax_Imperator_2%28loz%29.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Anax_Imperator_2(loz).JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Ecdysis" title="Ecdysis">Ecdysis</a>: a <a href="/wiki/Dragonfly" title="Dragonfly">dragonfly</a> has emerged from its dry <a href="/wiki/Exuviae" title="Exuviae">exuviae</a> and is expanding its wings. Like other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arthropods" title="Arthropods">arthropods</a>, its body is <a href="/wiki/Segmentation_(biology)" title="Segmentation (biology)">divided into segments</a>.</div></div></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ecdysozoa" title="Ecdysozoa">Ecdysozoa</a></div>\n<p>The Ecdysozoa are protostomes, named after their shared <a href="/wiki/Phenotypic_trait" title="Phenotypic trait">trait</a> of <a href="/wiki/Ecdysis" title="Ecdysis">ecdysis</a>, growth by moulting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[130]</a></sup> They include the largest animal phylum, the Arthropoda, which contains insects, spiders, crabs, and their kin. All of these have a body divided into <a href="/wiki/Segmentation_(biology)" title="Segmentation (biology)">repeating segments</a>, typically with paired appendages. Two smaller phyla, the <a href="/wiki/Onychophora" title="Onychophora">Onychophora</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tardigrada" title="Tardigrada">Tardigrada</a>, are close relatives of the arthropods and share these traits. The ecdysozoans also include the Nematoda or roundworms, perhaps the second largest animal phylum. Roundworms are typically microscopic, and occur in nearly every environment where there is water;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[131]</a></sup> some are important parasites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[132]</a></sup> Smaller phyla related to them are the <a href="/wiki/Nematomorpha" title="Nematomorpha">Nematomorpha</a> or horsehair worms, and the <a href="/wiki/Kinorhyncha" title="Kinorhyncha">Kinorhyncha</a>, <a href="/wiki/Priapulida" title="Priapulida">Priapulida</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Loricifera" title="Loricifera">Loricifera</a>. These groups have a reduced coelom, called a pseudocoelom.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[133]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Spiralia">Spiralia</span></h5>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Spiralia" title="Spiralia">Spiralia</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Spiral_cleavage_in_Trochus.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="629" data-file-width="2067" decoding="async" height="107" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Spiral_cleavage_in_Trochus.png/350px-Spiral_cleavage_in_Trochus.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Spiral_cleavage_in_Trochus.png/525px-Spiral_cleavage_in_Trochus.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Spiral_cleavage_in_Trochus.png/700px-Spiral_cleavage_in_Trochus.png 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Spiral_cleavage_in_Trochus.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spiral_cleavage" title="Spiral cleavage">Spiral cleavage</a> in a sea snail embryo</div></div></div>\n<p>The Spiralia are a large group of protostomes that develop by spiral cleavage in the early embryo.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shankland_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shankland-137">[134]</a></sup> The Spiralia\'s phylogeny has been disputed, but it contains a large clade, the superphylum <a href="/wiki/Lophotrochozoa" title="Lophotrochozoa">Lophotrochozoa</a>, and smaller groups of phyla such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rouphozoa" title="Rouphozoa">Rouphozoa</a> which includes the <a href="/wiki/Gastrotrich" title="Gastrotrich">gastrotrichs</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Flatworm" title="Flatworm">flatworms</a>. All of these are grouped as the <a href="/wiki/Platytrochozoa" title="Platytrochozoa">Platytrochozoa</a>, which has a sister group, the <a href="/wiki/Gnathifera_(clade)" title="Gnathifera (clade)">Gnathifera</a>, which includes the <a href="/wiki/Rotifer" title="Rotifer">rotifers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Struck2014_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-Struck2014-138">[135]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[136]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Lophotrochozoa includes the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mollusc" title="Mollusc">molluscs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Annelid" title="Annelid">annelids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brachiopod" title="Brachiopod">brachiopods</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nemertea" title="Nemertea">nemerteans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bryozoa" title="Bryozoa">bryozoa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Entoprocta" title="Entoprocta">entoprocts</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Struck2014_138-1"><a href="#cite_note-Struck2014-138">[135]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[137]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[138]</a></sup> The molluscs, the second-largest animal phylum by number of described species, includes <a href="/wiki/Snail" title="Snail">snails</a>, <a href="/wiki/Clam" title="Clam">clams</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Squid" title="Squid">squids</a>, while the annelids are the segmented worms, such as <a href="/wiki/Earthworm" title="Earthworm">earthworms</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lugworm" title="Lugworm">lugworms</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Leech" title="Leech">leeches</a>. These two groups have long been considered close relatives because they share <a href="/wiki/Trochophore" title="Trochophore">trochophore</a> larvae.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Giribet2000_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-Giribet2000-142">[139]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[140]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History_of_classification">History of classification</span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Taxonomy_(biology)" title="Taxonomy (biology)">Taxonomy (biology)</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_zoology_(through_1859)" title="History of zoology (through 1859)">History of zoology (through 1859)</a>, and <a href="/wiki/History_of_zoology_since_1859" title="History of zoology since 1859">History of zoology since 1859</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jean-Baptiste_de_Lamarck.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="843" data-file-width="668" decoding="async" height="215" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Jean-Baptiste_de_Lamarck.jpg/170px-Jean-Baptiste_de_Lamarck.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Jean-Baptiste_de_Lamarck.jpg/255px-Jean-Baptiste_de_Lamarck.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Jean-Baptiste_de_Lamarck.jpg/340px-Jean-Baptiste_de_Lamarck.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jean-Baptiste_de_Lamarck.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jean-Baptiste_de_Lamarck" title="Jean-Baptiste de Lamarck">Jean-Baptiste de Lamarck</a> led the creation of a modern classification of invertebrates, breaking up Linnaeus\'s "Vermes" into 9 phyla by 1809.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gould2011_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gould2011-144">[141]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>In the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Classical_era" title="Classical era">classical era</a>, Aristotle <a href="/wiki/Aristotle%27s_biology" title="Aristotle\'s biology">divided animals</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[d]</a></sup> based on his own observations, into those with blood (roughly, the vertebrates) and those without. The animals were then <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scala_naturae" title="Scala naturae">arranged on a scale</a> from man (with blood, 2 legs, rational soul) down through the live-bearing tetrapods (with blood, 4 legs, sensitive soul) and other groups such as crustaceans (no blood, many legs, sensitive soul) down to spontaneously-generating creatures like sponges (no blood, no legs, vegetable soul). <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> was uncertain whether sponges were animals, which in his system ought to have sensation, appetite, and locomotion, or plants, which did not: he knew that sponges could sense touch, and would contract if about to be pulled off their rocks, but that they were rooted like plants and never moved about.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[142]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1758, <a href="/wiki/Carl_Linnaeus" title="Carl Linnaeus">Carl Linnaeus</a> created the first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hierarchical" title="Hierarchical">hierarchical</a> classification in his <i><a href="/wiki/Systema_Naturae" title="Systema Naturae">Systema Naturae</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Linn1758_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-Linn1758-147">[143]</a></sup> In his original scheme, the animals were one of three kingdoms, divided into the classes of <a href="/wiki/Vermes_in_the_10th_edition_of_Systema_Naturae" title="Vermes in the 10th edition of Systema Naturae">Vermes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Insecta_in_the_10th_edition_of_Systema_Naturae" title="Insecta in the 10th edition of Systema Naturae">Insecta</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pisces_in_the_10th_edition_of_Systema_Naturae" title="Pisces in the 10th edition of Systema Naturae">Pisces</a>, <a href="/wiki/Amphibia_in_the_10th_edition_of_Systema_Naturae" title="Amphibia in the 10th edition of Systema Naturae">Amphibia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aves_in_the_10th_edition_of_Systema_Naturae" title="Aves in the 10th edition of Systema Naturae">Aves</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mammalia_in_the_10th_edition_of_Systema_Naturae" title="Mammalia in the 10th edition of Systema Naturae">Mammalia</a>. Since then the last four have all been subsumed into a single phylum, the <a href="/wiki/Chordate" title="Chordate">Chordata</a>, while his Insecta (which included the crustaceans and arachnids) and Vermes have been renamed or broken up. The process was begun in 1793 by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jean-Baptiste_de_Lamarck" title="Jean-Baptiste de Lamarck">Jean-Baptiste de Lamarck</a>, who called the Vermes <i>une esp&#232;ce de chaos</i> (a sort of chaos)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[e]</a></sup> and split the group into three new phyla, worms, echinoderms, and polyps (which contained corals and jellyfish). By 1809, in his <i><a href="/wiki/Philosophie_Zoologique" title="Philosophie Zoologique">Philosophie Zoologique</a></i>, Lamarck had created 9 phyla apart from vertebrates (where he still had 4 phyla: mammals, birds, reptiles, and fish) and molluscs, namely <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cirripede" title="Cirripede">cirripedes</a>, annelids, crustaceans, arachnids, insects, worms, <a href="/wiki/Radiata" title="Radiata">radiates</a>, polyps, and <a href="/wiki/Infusoria" title="Infusoria">infusorians</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gould2011_144-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gould2011-144">[141]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In his 1817 <i><a href="/wiki/Le_R%C3%A8gne_Animal" title="Le R&#232;gne Animal">Le R&#232;gne Animal</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Georges_Cuvier" title="Georges Cuvier">Georges Cuvier</a> used <a href="/wiki/Comparative_anatomy" title="Comparative anatomy">comparative anatomy</a> to group the animals into four <i>embranchements</i> ("branches" with different body plans, roughly corresponding to phyla), namely vertebrates, molluscs, articulated animals (arthropods and annelids), and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zoophytes" title="Zoophytes">zoophytes (radiata)</a> (echinoderms, cnidaria and other forms).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[145]</a></sup> This division into four was followed by the embryologist <a href="/wiki/Karl_Ernst_von_Baer" title="Karl Ernst von Baer">Karl Ernst von Baer</a> in 1828, the zoologist <a href="/wiki/Louis_Agassiz" title="Louis Agassiz">Louis Agassiz</a> in 1857, and the comparative anatomist <a href="/wiki/Richard_Owen" title="Richard Owen">Richard Owen</a> in 1860.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Valentine2004_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-Valentine2004-151">[146]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1874, <a href="/wiki/Ernst_Haeckel" title="Ernst Haeckel">Ernst Haeckel</a> divided the animal kingdom into two subkingdoms: Metazoa (multicellular animals, with five phyla: coelenterates, echinoderms, articulates, molluscs, and vertebrates) and Protozoa (single-celled animals), including a sixth animal phylum, sponges.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[147]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Valentine2004_151-1"><a href="#cite_note-Valentine2004-151">[146]</a></sup> The protozoa were later moved to the former kingdom <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protista" title="Protista">Protista</a>, leaving only the Metazoa as a synonym of Animalia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[148]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_human_culture">In human culture</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Carni_bovine_macellate.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="665" data-file-width="500" decoding="async" height="226" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Carni_bovine_macellate.JPG/170px-Carni_bovine_macellate.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Carni_bovine_macellate.JPG/255px-Carni_bovine_macellate.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Carni_bovine_macellate.JPG/340px-Carni_bovine_macellate.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Carni_bovine_macellate.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sides of <a href="/wiki/Beef" title="Beef">beef</a> in a <a href="/wiki/Slaughterhouse" title="Slaughterhouse">slaughterhouse</a></div></div></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Animals_in_culture" title="Animals in culture">Animals in culture</a></div>\n<p>The human population exploits a large number of other animal species for food, both of <a href="/wiki/Domestication_of_animals" title="Domestication of animals">domesticated</a> <a href="/wiki/Livestock" title="Livestock">livestock</a> species in <a href="/wiki/Animal_husbandry" title="Animal husbandry">animal husbandry</a> and, mainly at sea, by hunting wild species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FAOFish_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-FAOFish-154">[149]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Economist_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-Economist-155">[150]</a></sup> Marine fish of many species are <a href="/wiki/Fishing" title="Fishing">caught commercially</a> for food. A smaller number of species are <a href="/wiki/Fish_farming" title="Fish farming">farmed commercially</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FAOFish_154-1"><a href="#cite_note-FAOFish-154">[149]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[151]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[152]</a></sup>\nInvertebrates including <a href="/wiki/Cephalopod" title="Cephalopod">cephalopods</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crustacea" title="Crustacea">crustaceans</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bivalve" title="Bivalve">bivalve</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gastropod" title="Gastropod">gastropod</a> molluscs are hunted or farmed for food.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[153]</a></sup>\nChickens, cattle, sheep, pigs and other animals are raised as livestock for meat across the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Economist_155-1"><a href="#cite_note-Economist-155">[150]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[154]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[155]</a></sup>\nAnimal fibres such as wool are used to make textiles, while animal <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sinew" title="Sinew">sinews</a> have been used as lashings and bindings, and leather is widely used to make shoes and other items. Animals have been hunted and farmed for their fur to make items such as coats and hats.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[156]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[157]</a></sup> Dyestuffs including <a href="/wiki/Carmine" title="Carmine">carmine</a> (<a href="/wiki/Cochineal" title="Cochineal">cochineal</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[158]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[159]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Shellac" title="Shellac">shellac</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[160]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[161]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Kermes_(dye)" title="Kermes (dye)">kermes</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[162]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Munro214_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-Munro214-168">[163]</a></sup> have been made from the bodies of insects. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Working_animals" title="Working animals">Working animals</a> including cattle and horses have been used for work and transport from the first days of agriculture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pond2004_169-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pond2004-169">[164]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Animals such as the fruit fly <i><a href="/wiki/Drosophila_melanogaster" title="Drosophila melanogaster">Drosophila melanogaster</a></i> serve a major role in science as <a href="/wiki/Model_organism" title="Model organism">experimental models</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[165]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[166]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[167]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EUstatistics2013_173-0"><a href="#cite_note-EUstatistics2013-173">[168]</a></sup> Animals have been used to create <a href="/wiki/Vaccine" title="Vaccine">vaccines</a> since their discovery in the 18th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[169]</a></sup> Some medicines such as the cancer drug <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yondelis" title="Yondelis">Yondelis</a> are based on <a href="/wiki/Toxin" title="Toxin">toxins</a> or other molecules of animal origin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[170]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hebbuz.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3456" data-file-width="5184" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Hebbuz.JPG/220px-Hebbuz.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Hebbuz.JPG/330px-Hebbuz.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Hebbuz.JPG/440px-Hebbuz.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hebbuz.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Gun_dog" title="Gun dog">gun dog</a> retrieving a duck during a hunt</div></div></div>\n<p>People have used <a href="/wiki/Hunting_dog" title="Hunting dog">hunting dogs</a> to help chase down and retrieve animals,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[171]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Bird_of_prey" title="Bird of prey">birds of prey</a> to catch birds and mammals,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[172]</a></sup> while tethered <a href="/wiki/Cormorant" title="Cormorant">cormorants</a> have been <a href="/wiki/Cormorant_fishing" title="Cormorant fishing">used to catch fish</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-King2013_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-King2013-178">[173]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Poison_dart_frog" title="Poison dart frog">Poison dart frogs</a> have been used to poison the tips of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blowdart" title="Blowdart">blowpipe darts</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-amphibiaweb1_179-0"><a href="#cite_note-amphibiaweb1-179">[174]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[175]</a></sup>\nA wide variety of animals are kept as pets, from invertebrates such as tarantulas and octopuses, insects including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Praying_mantis" title="Praying mantis">praying mantises</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[176]</a></sup> reptiles such as <a href="/wiki/Snake" title="Snake">snakes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chameleon" title="Chameleon">chameleons</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[177]</a></sup> and birds including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Canary_(bird)" title="Canary (bird)">canaries</a>, <a href="/wiki/Parakeet" title="Parakeet">parakeets</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Parrot" title="Parrot">parrots</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[178]</a></sup> all finding a place. However, the most kept pet species are mammals, namely <a href="/wiki/Dog" title="Dog">dogs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cat" title="Cat">cats</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Rabbit" title="Rabbit">rabbits</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[179]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[180]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[181]</a></sup> There is a tension between the role of animals as companions to humans, and their existence as <a href="/wiki/Animal_rights" title="Animal rights">individuals with rights</a> of their own.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[182]</a></sup>\nA wide variety of terrestrial and aquatic animals are hunted <a href="/wiki/Animals_in_sport" title="Animals in sport">for sport</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[183]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Alexander_Coosemans_-_Still_Life_with_Lobster_and_Oysters.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="873" data-file-width="1149" decoding="async" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Alexander_Coosemans_-_Still_Life_with_Lobster_and_Oysters.jpg/220px-Alexander_Coosemans_-_Still_Life_with_Lobster_and_Oysters.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Alexander_Coosemans_-_Still_Life_with_Lobster_and_Oysters.jpg/330px-Alexander_Coosemans_-_Still_Life_with_Lobster_and_Oysters.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Alexander_Coosemans_-_Still_Life_with_Lobster_and_Oysters.jpg/440px-Alexander_Coosemans_-_Still_Life_with_Lobster_and_Oysters.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Alexander_Coosemans_-_Still_Life_with_Lobster_and_Oysters.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Artistic vision: <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Still_Life" title="Still Life">Still Life</a> with <a href="/wiki/Lobster" title="Lobster">Lobster</a> and <a href="/wiki/Oyster" title="Oyster">Oysters</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Coosemans" title="Alexander Coosemans">Alexander Coosemans</a>, c. 1660</div></div></div>\n<p>Animals have been the <a href="/wiki/Animal_style" title="Animal style">subjects of art</a> from the earliest times, both historical, as in <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Ancient Egypt</a>, and prehistoric, as in the <a href="/wiki/Lascaux" title="Lascaux">cave paintings at Lascaux</a>. Major animal paintings include <a href="/wiki/Albrecht_D%C3%BCrer" title="Albrecht D&#252;rer">Albrecht D&#252;rer</a>\'s 1515 <i><a href="/wiki/D%C3%BCrer%27s_Rhinoceros" title="D&#252;rer\'s Rhinoceros">The Rhinoceros</a></i>, and <a href="/wiki/George_Stubbs" title="George Stubbs">George Stubbs</a>\'s c. 1762 horse portrait <i><a href="/wiki/Whistlejacket" title="Whistlejacket">Whistlejacket</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jones_189-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jones-189">[184]</a></sup>\n<a href="/wiki/Arthropods_in_film" title="Arthropods in film">Insects</a>, birds and mammals play roles in literature and film,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190"><a href="#cite_note-190">[185]</a></sup> such as in giant bug movies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GregersdotterH&#246;glund2016_191-0"><a href="#cite_note-GregersdotterH&#246;glund2016-191">[186]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WarrenThomas2009_192-0"><a href="#cite_note-WarrenThomas2009-192">[187]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Crouse2008_193-0"><a href="#cite_note-Crouse2008-193">[188]</a></sup> Animals including <a href="/wiki/Insects_in_mythology" title="Insects in mythology">insects</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hearn_194-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hearn-194">[189]</a></sup> and mammals<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TFL_195-0"><a href="#cite_note-TFL-195">[190]</a></sup> feature in mythology and religion. In both Japan and Europe, a <a href="/wiki/Butterfly" title="Butterfly">butterfly</a> was seen as the personification of a person\'s soul,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hearn_194-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hearn-194">[189]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[191]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197"><a href="#cite_note-197">[192]</a></sup> while the <a href="/wiki/Scarab_(artifact)" title="Scarab (artifact)">scarab beetle</a> was sacred in ancient Egypt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198"><a href="#cite_note-198">[193]</a></sup> Among the mammals, <a href="/wiki/Cattle_in_religion_and_mythology" title="Cattle in religion and mythology">cattle</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[194]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Deer_in_mythology" title="Deer in mythology">deer</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TFL_195-1"><a href="#cite_note-TFL-195">[190]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Horse_worship" title="Horse worship">horses</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[195]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cultural_depictions_of_lions" title="Cultural depictions of lions">lions</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[196]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bat#Cultural_significance" title="Bat">bats</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReadGonzalez_202-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReadGonzalez-202">[197]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bear_worship" title="Bear worship">bears</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203"><a href="#cite_note-203">[198]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Wolves_in_folklore,_religion_and_mythology" title="Wolves in folklore, religion and mythology">wolves</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-204"><a href="#cite_note-204">[199]</a></sup> are the subjects of myths and worship. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Signs_of_the_zodiac" title="Signs of the zodiac">signs of the Western</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chinese_zodiac" title="Chinese zodiac">Chinese zodiacs</a> are based on animals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-205"><a href="#cite_note-205">[200]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tester1987_206-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tester1987-206">[201]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span></h2>\n<table class="metadata mbox-small" role="presentation" class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-69"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-69">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The application of <a href="/wiki/DNA_barcoding" title="DNA barcoding">DNA barcoding</a> to taxonomy further complicates this; a 2016 barcoding analysis estimated a total count of nearly 100,000 <a href="/wiki/Insect" title="Insect">insect</a> species for <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a> alone, and extrapolated that the global insect fauna must be in excess of 10 million species, of which nearly 2 million are in a single fly family known as gall midges (<a href="/wiki/Cecidomyiidae" title="Cecidomyiidae">Cecidomyiidae</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li specific and detailed model of a particular phylum with this general body plan.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-145"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-145">^</a></b></span> <span Animals</a></i>.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-149"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-149">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The prefix <i>une esp&#232;ce de</i> is pejorative.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[144]</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n</ol></div></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline"
